## Chapter: Theta Functions

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Functions, a fundamental concept in the field of geometric combinatorics. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'θ', are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, statistical mechanics, and combinatorics. They are particularly important in the study of lattices, which are discrete sets of points in a space that are used to model a variety of mathematical and physical phenomena.

Theta functions are defined as the sum of the exponential of the dot product of two vectors, where the vectors are taken from a lattice. This simple definition belies the complexity of the functions, which have a rich structure and many interesting properties. For instance, they are periodic functions, meaning that they repeat themselves after a certain period. This property is crucial in many applications, such as in the study of periodic functions in signal processing.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of theta functions, including their periodicity, symmetry, and the relationship between theta functions and other mathematical objects such as the Riemann zeta function. We will also discuss the applications of theta functions in various fields, including combinatorics, where they are used to count the number of solutions to certain types of equations.

We will also introduce the concept of theta series, which are series expansions of theta functions. These series are particularly useful in the study of lattices, as they provide a way to express the theta function as a sum of exponential terms. This representation is often easier to work with than the original definition, and it leads to many interesting results.

Finally, we will discuss the connection between theta functions and the theory of modular forms, which is a branch of number theory that deals with functions that are invariant under certain types of transformations. This connection is one of the most important aspects of theta functions, and it has led to many deep results in number theory and combinatorics.

In summary, this chapter will provide a comprehensive introduction to theta functions, covering their definition, properties, applications, and connections to other mathematical objects. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of theta functions and their role in geometric combinatorics.

## Chapter: Theta Functions

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Functions, a fundamental concept in the field of geometric combinatorics. Theta functions, named after the Greek letter 'θ', are mathematical functions that have been studied extensively in number theory, statistics, and combinatorics. They are particularly important in the study of lattices, which are discrete sets of points in a space that are used to model a variety of mathematical and physical phenomena.

Theta functions are defined as the ratio of two determinants, and they play a crucial role in the study of lattices. They are used to count the number of lattice points within a given region, a problem that is of central importance in many areas of mathematics. Theta functions are also closely related to the concept of quadratic forms, which are mathematical expressions of the form $ax^2 + bxy + cy^2$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of theta functions, their relationship with lattices and quadratic forms, and their applications in various areas of mathematics. We will also discuss the concept of theta series, which are infinite sums of theta functions, and their role in the study of modular forms.

We will begin by introducing the basic definitions and properties of theta functions, and then move on to more advanced topics such as the Jacobi theta function and the Riemann theta function. We will also discuss the concept of theta constants, which are constants that appear in the definitions of theta functions.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of theta functions and their role in geometric combinatorics. You will also be equipped with the necessary tools to explore more advanced topics in this fascinating field. So, let's embark on this mathematical journey together, exploring the intricacies of theta functions and their applications.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines. This theorem, named after the mathematicians who first proved it, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorics and has wide-ranging applications in various areas of mathematics.

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a powerful tool that allows us to determine the existence of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph. A monochromatic line is a set of edges that all have the same color. The theorem provides a necessary and sufficient condition for the existence of such lines, which is a crucial result in the study of colorings of graphs.

This chapter will be divided into several sections, each focusing on a different aspect of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem. We will begin by introducing the theorem and its statement, followed by a detailed proof. We will then explore the implications of the theorem, discussing its applications and generalizations. #### 4.1a Definition and Statement

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorics, named after the mathematicians who first proved it: Motzkin and Rabin. The theorem provides a necessary and sufficient condition for the existence of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph.

A monochromatic line is a set of edges that all have the same color. In the context of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem, we are interested in determining whether such lines exist in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph.

The theorem can be stated as follows:

**Theorem 4.1** (Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines): Let $G$ be a complete graph with $n$ vertices, and let $c$ be a coloring of the edges of $G$. Then there exists a monochromatic line in $G$ if and only if the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3.

This theorem is a powerful tool in the study of colorings of graphs. It provides a clear and concise condition for the existence of monochromatic lines, which can be used to determine the properties of the coloring.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the implications of this theorem, discussing its applications and generalizations. We will also provide a detailed proof of the theorem, which will help to solidify our understanding of its implications.

#### 4.1b Proof of Theorem

The proof of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a beautiful application of group theory and combinatorial arguments. We will provide a detailed proof of the theorem, which will help to solidify our understanding of its implications.

**Proof of Theorem 4.1** (Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines): Let $G$ be a complete graph with $n$ vertices, and let $c$ be a coloring of the edges of $G$. We will prove the theorem by showing that the existence of a monochromatic line is equivalent to the number of vertices with an odd degree being divisible by 3.

First, let's assume that there exists a monochromatic line in $G$. This means that there exists a set of edges $L$ that all have the same color. We can represent this line as a permutation $\pi$ of the vertices of $G$, where $\pi(v)$ is the vertex that is connected to $v$ by an edge in $L$.

Now, let's consider the group $G_L$ of all permutations of the vertices of $G$ that preserve the line $L$. This group is isomorphic to the symmetric group $S_n$, and its order is $n!$. We can also consider the subgroup $H$ of $G_L$ that consists of all even permutations. This subgroup has order $2^{n-1}(n/2)!$.

Since $G_L$ acts transitively on the vertices of $G$, the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3. This is because the number of vertices with an odd degree is equal to the number of orbits of the action of $G_L$ on the vertices of $G$, and each orbit has size divisible by 3.

Conversely, let's assume that the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3. This means that the group $H$ has a fixed point on the vertices of $G$. This fixed point corresponds to a monochromatic line in $G$, since any permutation in $H$ preserves the color of the edges in $L$.

Therefore, we have shown that the existence of a monochromatic line in $G$ is equivalent to the number of vertices with an odd degree being divisible by 3. This completes the proof of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines.

#### 4.1c Applications and Examples

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines has several interesting applications and examples that can help to further illustrate its implications. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and examples.

##### Example 1: The Complete Graph $K_n$

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be applied to the complete graph $K_n$ with $n$ vertices. In this case, the number of vertices with an odd degree is always divisible by 3, since the degree of each vertex is $n-1$, which is always divisible by 3 for $n \geq 3$. Therefore, there always exists a monochromatic line in $K_n$ for any coloring of its edges.

##### Example 2: The Cycle Graph $C_n$

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can also be applied to the cycle graph $C_n$ with $n$ vertices. In this case, the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3 if and only if $n$ is divisible by 3. This means that there exists a monochromatic line in $C_n$ for any coloring of its edges if and only if $n$ is divisible by 3.

##### Application: The Linear Programming Duality

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem has an interesting application in the field of linear programming duality. In particular, it can be used to prove the strong duality theorem for linear programming, which states that the optimal values of the primal and dual linear programming problems are equal. This proof is based on the idea of representing the primal and dual problems as colorings of the edges of a complete graph, and then applying the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem to show that there exists a monochromatic line in both colorings.

In conclusion, the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines has several interesting applications and examples that can help to further illustrate its implications. These applications and examples provide a deeper understanding of the theorem and its role in the field of combinatorics.




#### 4.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines is a beautiful application of group theory and combinatorial arguments. We will provide a detailed proof of the theorem, which will help to solidify our understanding of its implications.

**Proof of Theorem 4.1** (Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines): Let $G$ be a complete graph with $n$ vertices, and let $c$ be a coloring of the edges of $G$. We will prove the theorem by showing that the existence of a monochromatic line is equivalent to the number of vertices with an odd degree being divisible by 3.

First, we will prove the necessary condition. Suppose there exists a monochromatic line in $G$. This means that there exists a set of edges of the same color that form a line in the graph. Since the graph is complete, every vertex is incident to an edge of this line. Therefore, the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3.

Next, we will prove the sufficient condition. Suppose the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3. This means that there exists a set of vertices such that the number of vertices in this set is divisible by 3. We will color the edges between these vertices with one color and the remaining edges with another color. Since the number of vertices in this set is divisible by 3, there will be a monochromatic line in the graph.

This completes the proof of the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem on Monochromatic Lines. The theorem provides a necessary and sufficient condition for the existence of monochromatic lines in a given coloring of the edges of a complete graph. This theorem has many applications in the study of colorings of graphs and can be used to determine the properties of the coloring. The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem can be used to determine the existence of monochromatic lines in the colorings of complete graphs. For instance, consider the complete graph $K_n$ with $n$ vertices. If we color the edges of this graph with two colors, the theorem tells us that there exists a monochromatic line if and only if the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3.

For example, in the coloring of $K_7$ shown below, the number of vertices with an odd degree is 4, which is divisible by 3. Therefore, by the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem, there exists a monochromatic line in this coloring.

##### Example 2: Ramsey Theory

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem also has applications in Ramsey Theory, which is the study of structures that contain no small substructures of a certain type. In particular, the theorem can be used to prove the existence of monochromatic lines in the colorings of complete graphs, which is a fundamental result in Ramsey Theory.

For instance, consider the Ramsey number $R(3,3)$, which is the smallest number $n$ such that any coloring of the edges of the complete graph $K_n$ with two colors contains a monochromatic line. The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem tells us that $R(3,3) = 7$, since the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3 in any coloring of $K_7$.

##### Example 3: Graph Theory

The Motzkin-Rabin Theorem has many applications in graph theory. For instance, it can be used to determine the existence of monochromatic lines in the colorings of complete bipartite graphs.

Consider the complete bipartite graph $K_{m,n}$ with $m$ vertices on one side and $n$ vertices on the other. If we color the edges of this graph with two colors, the theorem tells us that there exists a monochromatic line if and only if the number of vertices with an odd degree is divisible by 3.

For example, in the coloring of $K_{3,4}$ shown below, the number of vertices with an odd degree is 4, which is divisible by 3. Therefore, by the Motzkin-Rabin Theorem, there exists a monochromatic line in this coloring.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem. This theorem, named after the Hungarian mathematician Endre Szemerédi and the American mathematician Ronald Trotter, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics, including number theory, graph theory, and computational geometry.

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a statement about the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects. It provides a way to understand the structure of these objects by looking at their projections onto lower-dimensional spaces. This is particularly useful in high-dimensional spaces, where traditional geometric intuition can be difficult to apply.

The theorem is named after the two mathematicians who first proved it, Endre Szemerédi and Ronald Trotter. Szemerédi, a Hungarian mathematician, is known for his work in number theory and combinatorics. He is particularly famous for his proof of the Szemerédi's Theorem, which states that every long enough arithmetic progression contains a prime number. Trotter, an American mathematician, is known for his work in combinatorial geometry and his contributions to the proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem.

In this chapter, we will first introduce the basic concepts and definitions needed to understand the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem. We will then state the theorem in its full generality and discuss its implications. We will also provide a sketch of the proof, highlighting the key ideas and techniques used. Finally, we will discuss some of the applications of the theorem in various areas of mathematics.

We hope that this chapter will provide a comprehensive introduction to the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem, equipping readers with the necessary tools to understand and apply this powerful result in their own research.




### Subsection: 5.1a Definition and Statement

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects, and has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics, including number theory, graph theory, and computational geometry.

#### Definition

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is named after the two mathematicians who first proved it, Endre Szemerédi and Ronald Trotter. Szemerédi, a Hungarian mathematician, is known for his work in number theory and combinatorics. He is particularly famous for his proof of the Szemerédi's Theorem, which states that every long enough arithmetic progression contains a prime number. Trotter, an American mathematician, is known for his work in combinatorial geometry and his contributions to the proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem.

The theorem is a statement about the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects. It provides a way to understand the structure of these objects by looking at their projections onto lower-dimensional spaces. This is particularly useful in high-dimensional spaces, where traditional geometric intuition can be difficult to apply.

#### Statement

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem can be stated as follows:

Given a set of $n$ points in $d$-dimensional space, there exists a subspace of dimension $k$ such that the number of points in this subspace is at least $n/2^k$.

This theorem provides a lower bound on the number of points in a subspace of a given dimension. This lower bound is particularly useful in high-dimensional spaces, where traditional geometric intuition can be difficult to apply.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the implications of this theorem and provide a sketch of the proof.




#### 5.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a complex and intricate process that involves a series of steps and derivations. In this section, we will outline the main steps of the proof and provide a brief explanation of each.

##### Step 1: Reduction to the Case of a Finite Set

The first step in the proof is to reduce the problem to the case of a finite set. This is done by considering the set of all points in $d$-dimensional space that are within a certain distance $r$ of the given set of points. This set is finite, and its size is bounded by a constant multiple of the size of the original set. This reduction allows us to focus on the finite set without losing any essential information.

##### Step 2: Introduction of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a powerful tool for understanding the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects. It provides a way to understand the structure of these objects by looking at their projections onto lower-dimensional spaces. This is particularly useful in high-dimensional spaces, where traditional geometric intuition can be difficult to apply.

##### Step 3: Proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem

The proof of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem involves a series of steps and derivations. The proof begins by considering a set of points in $d$-dimensional space, and then proceeds to show that there exists a subspace of dimension $k$ such that the number of points in this subspace is at least $n/2^k$. This is done by considering the projections of the points onto lower-dimensional spaces and using the properties of these projections to derive the desired result.

##### Step 4: Conclusion

The conclusion of the proof is a statement about the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects. It provides a way to understand the structure of these objects by looking at their projections onto lower-dimensional spaces. This is particularly useful in high-dimensional spaces, where traditional geometric intuition can be difficult to apply.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the implications of this theorem and provide a sketch of the proof.

#### 5.1c Applications and Examples

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the theorem in action.

##### Example 1: High-Dimensional Geometric Objects

One of the most common applications of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is in the study of high-dimensional geometric objects. These objects are often difficult to visualize and understand due to their high dimensionality. The theorem provides a way to understand the structure of these objects by looking at their projections onto lower-dimensional spaces. This can be particularly useful in situations where traditional geometric intuition is difficult to apply.

For example, consider a set of points in $d$-dimensional space. The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem guarantees that there exists a subspace of dimension $k$ such that the number of points in this subspace is at least $n/2^k$. This can be used to show that the set of points is not too spread out in any one direction, which can be useful in various applications.

##### Example 2: Number Theory

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem also has applications in number theory. In particular, it is used in the study of primes and arithmetic progressions. The theorem can be used to show that every long enough arithmetic progression contains a prime number. This is a result known as Szemerédi's Theorem, which is a special case of the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem.

For example, consider an arithmetic progression of length $n$. The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem guarantees that there exists a subspace of dimension $k$ such that the number of points in this subspace is at least $n/2^k$. If we choose $k$ to be large enough, this means that the arithmetic progression must contain a prime number.

##### Example 3: Graph Theory

The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem also has applications in graph theory. In particular, it is used in the study of graph coloring problems. The theorem can be used to show that every graph has a coloring with at most $n/2^k$ colors, where $k$ is the dimension of the subspace.

For example, consider a graph with $n$ vertices. The Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem guarantees that there exists a subspace of dimension $k$ such that the number of vertices in this subspace is at least $n/2^k$. This can be used to show that the graph has a coloring with at most $n/2^k$ colors.

In conclusion, the Szemerédi-Trotter Theorem is a powerful tool with numerous applications in various areas of mathematics. Its ability to understand the structure of high-dimensional geometric objects makes it a valuable tool in the study of these objects.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, exploring the concepts of unit distances, unit area triangles, Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture. These topics are fundamental to understanding the geometric properties of points in the plane and their implications for combinatorial structures.

We will begin by discussing unit distances, a concept that is central to many problems in combinatorial geometry. Unit distances refer to the distances between points in a set, where each distance is normalized to be either 0 or 1. This normalization allows us to capture the relative positions of points in a set, and it is a key tool in many proofs and constructions in combinatorial geometry.

Next, we will explore unit area triangles, a concept that is closely related to unit distances. A unit area triangle is a triangle whose area is normalized to be either 0 or 1. Like unit distances, unit area triangles provide a way to capture the relative sizes of triangles in a set. We will see how these concepts are interconnected and how they can be used to prove important theorems.

One such theorem is Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, which provides a powerful tool for proving the existence of certain geometric structures. This theorem states that if a set of points in the plane has the property that the distance between any two points is either 0 or 1, then there exists a line that separates the points into two sets, such that the distance between any two points in the same set is either 0 or 1. This theorem has many applications in combinatorial geometry, including in the proof of the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a fundamental problem in combinatorial geometry that has been studied extensively. It asks whether every set of $n$ points in the plane can be partitioned into $n$ lines such that no three lines intersect in a single point. This conjecture is closely related to the concepts of unit distances and unit area triangles, and we will explore this relationship in detail.

Throughout this chapter, we will provide numerous examples and illustrations to help you understand these concepts and their applications. We will also provide references to the original sources for these concepts, so that you can delve deeper into these topics if you wish.

So, let's embark on this journey into the world of geometric combinatorics, exploring the fascinating interplay between geometry and combinatorics.




### Subsection: 6.1a Definitions and Statements

#### 6.1a.1 Unit Distances

Unit distances are a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. They refer to the distances between points in a set, where each distance is normalized to be either 0 or 1. This normalization allows us to capture the relative positions of points in a set, and it is a key tool in many proofs and constructions in combinatorial geometry.

Mathematically, let $S$ be a set of points in the plane. For any two points $p, q \in S$, we define the unit distance $d(p, q)$ between $p$ and $q$ as follows:

$$
d(p, q) = \begin{cases}
0, & \text{if } p = q \\
1, & \text{if } p \neq q
\end{cases}
$$

This definition ensures that the distance between any two points in $S$ is either 0 or 1. This property is crucial in many of the theorems and proofs we will explore in this chapter.

#### 6.1a.2 Unit Area Triangles

Unit area triangles are another fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. They are closely related to unit distances and provide a way to capture the relative sizes of triangles in a set.

A unit area triangle is a triangle whose area is normalized to be either 0 or 1. Mathematically, let $T$ be a set of triangles in the plane. For any triangle $t \in T$, we define the unit area $A(t)$ of $t$ as follows:

$$
A(t) = \begin{cases}
0, & \text{if } t \text{ is degenerate} \\
1, & \text{if } t \text{ is non-degenerate}
\end{cases}
$$

where a triangle $t$ is degenerate if it has an area of 0, and non-degenerate if it has an area of at least 1. This definition ensures that the area of any triangle in $T$ is either 0 or 1. This property is crucial in many of the theorems and proofs we will explore in this chapter.

#### 6.1a.3 Beck's Two Extremities Theorem

Beck's Two Extremities Theorem is a powerful tool for proving the existence of certain geometric structures. It states that if a set of points in the plane has the property that the distance between any two points is either 0 or 1, then there exists a line that separates the points into two sets, such that the distance between any two points in the same set is either 0 or 1.

Mathematically, let $S$ be a set of points in the plane such that $d(p, q) \in \{0, 1\}$ for all $p, q \in S$. Then there exists a line $l$ such that $S \setminus l$ can be partitioned into two sets $S_1$ and $S_2$, where $d(p, q) \in \{0, 1\}$ for all $p, q \in S_1$ and $p, q \in S_2$.

#### 6.1a.4 Weak Dirac Conjecture

The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a fundamental problem in combinatorial geometry that has been studied extensively. It asks whether every set of $n$ points in the plane can be partitioned into $n$ lines such that no three lines intersect in a single point.

Mathematically, let $S$ be a set of $n$ points in the plane. The Weak Dirac Conjecture asks whether there exists a partition of $S$ into $n$ lines such that no three lines intersect in a single point.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these concepts and theorems for the study of geometric combinatorics.




#### 6.1b Proofs and Derivations

In this section, we will delve into the proofs and derivations of the concepts and theorems introduced in the previous section. We will start with Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, followed by the proof of the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

#### 6.1b.1 Beck's Two Extremities Theorem

Beck's Two Extremities Theorem is a powerful tool for proving the existence of certain geometric structures. It states that if a set of points in the plane has the property that the distance between any two points is either 0 or 1, then there exists a line segment connecting two of these points whose length is 1.

Proof:

Let $S$ be a set of points in the plane such that the distance between any two points is either 0 or 1. We will prove the existence of a line segment of length 1 by contradiction.

Assume the contrary, that for every pair of points $p, q \in S$, the distance $d(p, q) \neq 1$. This means that for every pair of points, the distance between them is either 0 or less than 1.

Consider the set $T$ of all line segments connecting points in $S$. Each line segment in $T$ has a length that is either 0 or less than 1. Since the set $S$ is finite, the set $T$ is also finite.

Let $L$ be the longest line segment in $T$. The length of $L$ is either 0 or less than 1. If the length of $L$ is 0, then $L$ is a single point, which is a contradiction. Therefore, the length of $L$ is less than 1.

Let $p$ and $q$ be the endpoints of $L$. The distance $d(p, q) \leq 1$ since the length of $L$ is less than 1. However, since the distance between any two points in $S$ is either 0 or 1, we have $d(p, q) = 1$, which is a contradiction.

Therefore, there exists a line segment of length 1 connecting two points in $S$. This proves Beck's Two Extremities Theorem.

#### 6.1b.2 Proof of the Weak Dirac Conjecture

The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a fundamental result in combinatorial geometry. It states that for any set of $n$ points in the plane, there exists a line segment of length at least $\frac{1}{n}$.

Proof:

Let $S$ be a set of $n$ points in the plane. We will prove the existence of a line segment of length at least $\frac{1}{n}$ by contradiction.

Assume the contrary, that for every pair of points $p, q \in S$, the distance $d(p, q) < \frac{1}{n}$. This means that for every pair of points, the distance between them is less than $\frac{1}{n}$.

Consider the set $T$ of all line segments connecting points in $S$. Each line segment in $T$ has a length that is less than $\frac{1}{n}$. Since the set $S$ is finite, the set $T$ is also finite.

Let $L$ be the longest line segment in $T$. The length of $L$ is less than $\frac{1}{n}$. Let $p$ and $q$ be the endpoints of $L$. The distance $d(p, q) < \frac{1}{n}$ since the length of $L$ is less than $\frac{1}{n}$. However, since the distance between any two points in $S$ is less than $\frac{1}{n}$, we have $d(p, q) = \frac{1}{n}$, which is a contradiction.

Therefore, there exists a line segment of length at least $\frac{1}{n}$ connecting two points in $S$. This proves the Weak Dirac Conjecture.




#### 6.1c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some applications and examples of the concepts and theorems introduced in the previous sections. We will start with the applications of Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, followed by some examples of the Weak Dirac Conjecture.

#### 6.1c.1 Applications of Beck's Two Extremities Theorem

Beck's Two Extremities Theorem has found numerous applications in various fields, including computer science, geometry, and combinatorics. One of the most significant applications is in the design of algorithms for solving optimization problems.

Consider a set of points in the plane representing the vertices of a graph. The distance between any two points represents the cost of traveling from one vertex to another. Beck's Two Extremities Theorem can be used to find the shortest path between two vertices, which is a fundamental problem in graph theory.

Another application is in the design of data structures. For example, the Simple Function Point method, a software estimation technique, uses the concept of unit distances to estimate the size of a software system. The method assigns a unit distance to each element of the system, and the total size of the system is calculated as the sum of these distances.

#### 6.1c.2 Examples of the Weak Dirac Conjecture

The Weak Dirac Conjecture has been applied to various problems in combinatorial geometry. One of the most notable applications is in the study of unit area triangles.

A unit area triangle is a triangle whose area is 1. The Weak Dirac Conjecture can be used to prove that for any set of $n$ points in the plane, there exists a unit area triangle whose vertices are among these points. This result has been used in the study of convex polyhedra and in the design of algorithms for solving geometric problems.

Another example of the application of the Weak Dirac Conjecture is in the study of the TELCOMP project. TELCOMP is a project aimed at developing a comprehensive theory of elementary logic. The Weak Dirac Conjecture has been used to prove the existence of certain geometric structures in the project, which has been instrumental in the development of the theory.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta-Graphs, Theta-Series, Theta-Functions

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta-Graphs, Theta-Series, and Theta-Functions, three fundamental concepts in the field of geometric combinatorics. These concepts are not only mathematically intriguing but also have wide-ranging applications in various fields such as computer science, physics, and engineering.

Theta-Graphs, also known as theta graphs or theta grids, are a type of graph that are particularly useful in the study of combinatorial geometry. They are defined as the intersection of two lattices, and their vertices represent the integer points in the plane. Theta-graphs have been extensively studied due to their unique properties and their connections to other mathematical objects such as polyhedra and tilings.

Theta-Series are mathematical series that are defined in terms of theta-graphs. They are a generalization of the well-known Dirichlet series and have been used in various areas of mathematics, including number theory, combinatorics, and statistical mechanics. Theta-series are particularly interesting because of their ability to capture the structure of theta-graphs and their connections to other mathematical objects.

Theta-Functions, also known as theta functions or Jacobi theta functions, are a class of functions that are defined in terms of theta-graphs. They are a generalization of the well-known sine and cosine functions and have been used in various areas of mathematics, including complex analysis, differential equations, and number theory. Theta-functions are particularly interesting because of their ability to capture the structure of theta-graphs and their connections to other mathematical objects.

In this chapter, we will explore these concepts in depth, starting with the basics and gradually moving on to more advanced topics. We will also discuss their applications and connections to other mathematical objects. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Theta-Graphs, Theta-Series, and Theta-Functions, and be able to apply these concepts to solve problems in geometric combinatorics.




#### 6.2a Lemma Statement

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a fundamental result in the study of geometric combinatorics. It provides a powerful tool for analyzing the structure of multigraphs, which are graphs that allow multiple edges between any pair of vertices. The lemma is particularly useful in the study of unit distances and unit area triangles, as it provides a way to systematically construct these objects.

#### 6.2a.1 Statement of the Lemma

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs can be stated as follows:

Given a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$, and a subset $S \subseteq V$, there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

In other words, for every edge in the submultigraph $G'$, there exists a path that connects the two vertices of the edge without crossing any other edge in $G'$. This property is known as the crossing property.

#### 6.2a.2 Proof of the Lemma

The proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is based on the concept of a crossing sequence. A crossing sequence is a sequence of edges in $E'$ such that for each pair of consecutive edges $e_1, e_2$ in the sequence, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

The proof begins by choosing an arbitrary edge $e_1 \in E'$. The path from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$ is then constructed. This path is denoted by $P_1$.

The proof then proceeds by induction on the length of the crossing sequence. Assuming that a crossing sequence of length $k$ has been constructed, the proof shows how to extend this sequence to a crossing sequence of length $k+1$. This is done by choosing an edge $e_{k+1} \in E'$ that does not cross any edge in the current crossing sequence, and constructing a path $P_{k+1}$ from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

The proof concludes by showing that the constructed crossing sequence is in fact a path from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. This completes the proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs.

#### 6.2a.3 Applications of the Lemma

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has numerous applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. One of the most significant applications is in the construction of unit distances and unit area triangles.

For example, consider a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$. The Crossing Lemma can be used to construct a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S \subseteq V$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. This submultigraph $G'$ can then be used to construct a unit distance or a unit area triangle, depending on the specific properties of the edges in $E'$.

In conclusion, the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a powerful tool in the study of geometric combinatorics. Its applications are vast and varied, making it an essential result for any comprehensive study of this field.

#### 6.2b Proof of the Lemma

The proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is based on the concept of a crossing sequence. A crossing sequence is a sequence of edges in $E'$ such that for each pair of consecutive edges $e_1, e_2$ in the sequence, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

The proof begins by choosing an arbitrary edge $e_1 \in E'$. The path from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$ is then constructed. This path is denoted by $P_1$.

The proof then proceeds by induction on the length of the crossing sequence. Assuming that a crossing sequence of length $k$ has been constructed, the proof shows how to extend this sequence to a crossing sequence of length $k+1$. This is done by choosing an edge $e_{k+1} \in E'$ that does not cross any edge in the current crossing sequence, and constructing a path $P_{k+1}$ from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

The proof concludes by showing that the constructed crossing sequence is in fact a path from $u$ to $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. This completes the proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs.

#### 6.2c Applications and Examples

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has numerous applications in the study of geometric combinatorics. One of the most significant applications is in the construction of unit distances and unit area triangles.

For example, consider a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$. The Crossing Lemma can be used to construct a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S \subseteq V$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. This submultigraph $G'$ can then be used to construct a unit distance or a unit area triangle, depending on the specific properties of the edges in $E'$.

Another application of the Crossing Lemma is in the study of the Weak Dirac Conjecture. The Weak Dirac Conjecture is a conjecture in graph theory that states that for every graph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$, there exists a subgraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S \subseteq V$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. The Crossing Lemma provides a way to construct such a subgraph $G'$.

The Crossing Lemma also has applications in the study of the Beck's Two Extremities Theorem. The Beck's Two Extremities Theorem is a theorem in combinatorial geometry that states that for every set of $n$ points in the plane, there exists a line that passes through exactly two of these points. The Crossing Lemma can be used to construct such a line.

In conclusion, the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a powerful tool in the study of geometric combinatorics. It has numerous applications in the construction of unit distances, unit area triangles, and in the study of various theorems and conjectures in combinatorial geometry.

We then moved on to Beck’s Two Extremities Theorem. This theorem states that for any set of $n$ points in the plane, there exists a line that passes through exactly two of these points. This theorem is a powerful tool in combinatorics and has many applications.

Finally, we explored the Weak Dirac Conjecture. This conjecture is a fundamental problem in combinatorics. It states that for any set of $n$ points in the plane, there exists a line that passes through at least $n/2$ of these points. This conjecture is still open, but it has been proven for certain special cases.

In conclusion, this chapter has provided a comprehensive study of unit distances, unit area triangles, Beck’s Two Extremities Theorem, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture. These concepts are fundamental to the study of geometric combinatorics and have wide-ranging applications in various areas of mathematics.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta Graphs

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Graphs, a fundamental concept in the study of geometric combinatorics. Theta Graphs, named after the Greek letter 'Theta', are a type of graph that are used to represent and analyze various geometric structures. They are particularly useful in the study of convex polyhedra, where they provide a powerful tool for understanding the structure and properties of these objects.

Theta Graphs are a type of bipartite graph, meaning that they can be divided into two disjoint sets of vertices such that all edges connect a vertex from one set to a vertex from the other. In the context of convex polyhedra, these two sets of vertices typically represent the vertices and edges of the polyhedron. The edges of the Theta Graph correspond to the edges of the polyhedron, and the vertices represent the vertices and edges of the polyhedron.

One of the key properties of Theta Graphs is that they are always planar, meaning that they can be drawn in the plane without any edges crossing each other. This property is closely related to the fact that convex polyhedra can always be embedded in three-dimensional space in such a way that no two of their faces intersect.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of Theta Graphs, their construction, and their applications in the study of convex polyhedra. We will also discuss the concept of Theta Graphs in the context of the Weak Dirac Conjecture, a fundamental problem in combinatorics that asks whether every graph contains a line that passes through at least half of its vertices.

As we journey through the world of Theta Graphs, we will see how these simple, yet powerful, graphs provide a deep understanding of the structure and properties of geometric objects. We will also see how they are intertwined with some of the most fundamental problems and concepts in combinatorics.




#### 6.2b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is a direct consequence of the Crossing Sequence Lemma, which we will prove first.

#### 6.2b.1 Proof of the Crossing Sequence Lemma

The Crossing Sequence Lemma can be stated as follows:

Given a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$, and a subset $S \subseteq V$, there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

Proof:

We prove this lemma by induction on the number of edges in $E'$. If $E' = \emptyset$, then the lemma is trivially true. Now, assume that the lemma is true for all submultigraphs with at most $k$ edges, and consider a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that $|E'| = k + 1$.

Choose an arbitrary edge $e_1 \in E'$. By the induction hypothesis, there exists a submultigraph $G_1 \subseteq G'$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E_1 \subseteq E'$ such that for every edge $e \in E_1$, there exists a path in $G_1$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E_1$.

Now, consider the submultigraph $G_2 = G_1 \setminus \{e_1\}$. By the induction hypothesis, there exists a submultigraph $G_2 \subseteq G'$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E_2 \subseteq E'$ such that for every edge $e \in E_2$, there exists a path in $G_2$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E_2$.

Continuing this process, we can construct a sequence of submultigraphs $G_1, G_2, \ldots, G_{k+1}$ such that for each $i \in \{1, \ldots, k+1\}$, there exists a submultigraph $G_i \subseteq G'$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E_i \subseteq E'$ such that for every edge $e \in E_i$, there exists a path in $G_i$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E_i$.

This completes the proof of the Crossing Sequence Lemma, and hence the proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs.

#### 6.2b.2 Derivations of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs can be derived from the Crossing Sequence Lemma. The proof is as follows:

Given a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$, and a subset $S \subseteq V$, we want to show that there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

By the Crossing Sequence Lemma, there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

Now, consider an arbitrary edge $e \in E'$. By the Crossing Sequence Lemma, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. This path is a crossing sequence for the edge $e$. Therefore, the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is true.

This completes the proof of the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs.




#### 6.2c Applications and Examples

The Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs has several applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the lemma in action.

#### 6.2c.1 Applications in Computer Science

In computer science, the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is used in the design and analysis of algorithms. For instance, it is used in the design of algorithms for graph traversal, where the lemma is used to ensure that the traversal does not cross itself. The lemma is also used in the analysis of algorithms for network routing, where it is used to ensure that the routing does not cross itself.

#### 6.2c.2 Applications in Mathematics

In mathematics, the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs is used in the study of multigraphs and their properties. For instance, it is used in the study of the crossing number of a multigraph, which is the minimum number of crossings in any drawing of the multigraph. The lemma is also used in the study of the crossing sequence of a multigraph, which is a sequence of crossings in a drawing of the multigraph.

#### 6.2c.3 Examples

Consider a multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$. Let $S \subseteq V$ be a subset of the vertices. By the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs, there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

For instance, consider the multigraph $G$ in Figure 1. The vertex set $V$ is $\{a, b, c, d, e\}$, and the edge set $E$ is $\{ab, ac, ad, be, bd, ce, cd, de\}$. Let $S = \{a, b, c, d\}$. By the Crossing Lemma for Multigraphs, there exists a submultigraph $G' \subseteq G$ with vertex set $S$ and edge set $E' \subseteq E$ such that for every edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

In this example, the submultigraph $G'$ is the subgraph induced by the vertices $a$, $b$, $c$, and $d$. The edge set $E'$ is $\{ab, ac, ad, be, bd, ce, cd, de\}$. For each edge $e \in E'$, there exists a path in $G'$ from the vertex $u$ to the vertex $v$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$. For instance, for the edge $ab$, there exists a path from $a$ to $b$ that does not cross any other edge in $E'$.

Figure 1: A multigraph $G$ with vertex set $V$ and edge set $E$.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Theta Graphs, Theta Bound, Theta Function

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of Theta Graphs, Theta Bound, and Theta Function, three fundamental concepts in the field of geometric combinatorics. These concepts are not only mathematically intriguing but also have wide-ranging applications in various fields such as computer science, engineering, and physics.

Theta Graphs, also known as theta networks, are a class of graphs that have been extensively studied in the literature. They are characterized by their unique structure, which makes them particularly useful in the study of combinatorial geometry. Theta Graphs have been used in a variety of applications, including network design, graph theory, and computational geometry.

The Theta Bound, on the other hand, is a powerful tool in combinatorial geometry that provides a lower bound on the number of vertices in a graph. It is named after the Greek letter theta ($\theta$), which is used to denote the bound. The Theta Bound has been instrumental in the study of various geometric problems, including the study of convex polyhedra and the design of efficient algorithms.

Lastly, the Theta Function, also known as the Jacobi Theta Function, is a mathematical function that plays a crucial role in the study of elliptic curves and modular forms. It is defined as a series and has been extensively studied in the literature due to its many interesting properties. The Theta Function has found applications in various fields, including number theory, statistical mechanics, and quantum mechanics.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore these concepts in depth, starting with an introduction to Theta Graphs, followed by a discussion on the Theta Bound, and finally, an in-depth study of the Theta Function. We will also discuss the interconnections between these concepts and their applications in various fields. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of these concepts and their role in geometric combinatorics.




### Subsection: 6.3a Problem Statement

In this section, we will explore the minimum number of distinct distances problem, which is a fundamental problem in geometric combinatorics. This problem is concerned with determining the minimum number of distinct distances that can be formed by a set of points in the plane. 

#### 6.3a.1 Definition of Distinct Distances

A distance between two points $p$ and $q$ in the plane is said to be distinct if it is not equal to any other distance between any other pair of points in the plane. In other words, a distance is distinct if it is unique. 

#### 6.3a.2 Problem Statement

Given a set of $n$ points in the plane, what is the minimum number of distinct distances that can be formed by these points? 

#### 6.3a.3 Examples

Consider a set of three points $A$, $B$, and $C$ in the plane. The distances $AB$, $BC$, and $CA$ are distinct, as they are unique. However, if we add a fourth point $D$ to this set, the distances $AD$, $BD$, and $CD$ are not distinct, as they are not unique. 

In general, the minimum number of distinct distances formed by a set of $n$ points in the plane is $n(n-1)/2$. This is because each pair of points contributes a distinct distance, and there are $n(n-1)/2$ pairs of points. 

In the next section, we will explore the implications of this problem and its applications in various fields.




#### 6.3b Solutions and Approaches

In this section, we will explore some of the solutions and approaches to the minimum number of distinct distances problem. 

#### 6.3b.1 Brute Force Approach

The brute force approach to solving this problem involves systematically checking all possible pairs of points and determining whether the distances between them are distinct. This approach is straightforward but can be computationally intensive for large sets of points. 

#### 6.3b.2 Geometric Approach

A geometric approach to this problem involves using the properties of circles and lines to determine the number of distinct distances. For example, if we consider a set of points on a circle, the distances between any two points on the circle are all equal. Therefore, the minimum number of distinct distances in this case is $n(n-1)/2 - n = (n-1)(n-2)/2$. 

#### 6.3b.3 Algorithmic Approach

An algorithmic approach to this problem involves using dynamic programming to solve the problem in polynomial time. This approach involves breaking down the problem into smaller subproblems and storing the solutions to these subproblems in a table. The solution to the original problem can then be obtained by combining the solutions to the subproblems. 

#### 6.3b.4 Applications of the Minimum Number of Distinct Distances Problem

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has many applications in various fields. For example, in computer graphics, it is used to determine the minimum number of distinct distances needed to uniquely identify a point in space. In network design, it is used to determine the minimum number of distinct distances needed to uniquely identify a node in a network. In combinatorial optimization, it is used to determine the minimum number of distinct distances needed to uniquely identify a solution to a combinatorial optimization problem.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the minimum number of distinct distances problem in more detail.

#### 6.3c Implications and Consequences

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has several implications and consequences that are worth exploring. These implications and consequences are not only interesting from a theoretical perspective, but also have practical applications in various fields.

#### 6.3c.1 Implications of the Minimum Number of Distinct Distances Problem

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has several implications that are worth exploring. These implications are not only interesting from a theoretical perspective, but also have practical applications in various fields.

##### Implication 1: Uniqueness of Point Identification

The minimum number of distinct distances problem is closely related to the problem of uniquely identifying a point in space. In fact, the minimum number of distinct distances needed to uniquely identify a point in space is equal to the minimum number of distinct distances in a set of points. This property is useful in various fields, such as computer graphics, where it is often necessary to uniquely identify a point in space.

##### Implication 2: Complexity of Distance Computation

The minimum number of distinct distances problem also has implications for the complexity of distance computation. In particular, the brute force approach to solving this problem involves systematically checking all possible pairs of points and determining whether the distances between them are distinct. This approach is computationally intensive and can be a bottleneck in applications that require large-scale distance computation.

##### Implication 3: Geometric Properties of Sets of Points

The minimum number of distinct distances problem also has implications for the geometric properties of sets of points. For example, the geometric approach to this problem involves using the properties of circles and lines to determine the number of distinct distances. This approach can provide insights into the structure of sets of points and can be useful in various fields, such as geometry and topology.

#### 6.3c.2 Consequences of the Minimum Number of Distinct Distances Problem

The minimum number of distinct distances problem also has several consequences that are worth exploring. These consequences are not only interesting from a theoretical perspective, but also have practical applications in various fields.

##### Consequence 1: Efficient Algorithms for Distance Computation

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has led to the development of efficient algorithms for distance computation. For example, the algorithmic approach to this problem involves using dynamic programming to solve the problem in polynomial time. This approach can be used to efficiently compute distances in large sets of points, making it useful in various fields, such as data analysis and machine learning.

##### Consequence 2: Insights into the Structure of Sets of Points

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has also led to insights into the structure of sets of points. For example, the geometric approach to this problem involves using the properties of circles and lines to determine the number of distinct distances. This approach can provide insights into the structure of sets of points and can be useful in various fields, such as geometry and topology.

##### Consequence 3: Applications in Various Fields

The minimum number of distinct distances problem has applications in various fields, such as computer graphics, data analysis, and machine learning. These applications are not only interesting from a theoretical perspective, but also have practical implications in these fields. For example, the minimum number of distinct distances problem is used in computer graphics to uniquely identify a point in space, in data analysis to efficiently compute distances, and in machine learning to understand the structure of sets of points.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on unit distances, unit area triangles, Beck’s Two Extremities Theorem, and the Weak Dirac Conjecture. We have delved into the intricacies of these concepts, understanding their definitions, properties, and applications.

We began by examining unit distances, a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. We learned that a unit distance is the shortest distance between two points in a given space. This concept is crucial in many areas of mathematics, including geometry, topology, and computer science.

Next, we explored unit area triangles, another fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics. We learned that a unit area triangle is a triangle with an area of 1. This concept is particularly important in the study of triangulations and the calculation of surface areas.

We then moved on to Beck's Two Extremities Theorem, a powerful result in geometric combinatorics. This theorem provides a characterization of the vertices of a convex polytope in terms of their extreme points. This theorem has numerous applications in combinatorial optimization and computational geometry.

Finally, we discussed the Weak Dirac Conjecture, a conjecture in geometric combinatorics that has been a subject of intense study for many years. This conjecture provides a lower bound on the number of vertices of a graph with a given number of edges.

Throughout this chapter, we have seen how these concepts are interconnected and how they form the backbone of geometric combinatorics. We have also seen how these concepts have been applied in various areas of mathematics and computer science.

In conclusion, the study of geometric combinatorics is a rich and rewarding field that offers many opportunities for exploration and discovery. The concepts discussed in this chapter provide a solid foundation for further study in this area.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics, specifically focusing on the Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves. This theorem, named after the Hungarian mathematicians Miklos Pach and Shimon Sharir, is a fundamental result in the field of combinatorial geometry. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the incidences between points and combinatorial curves in geometric configurations.

The Pach-Sharir Theorem is a cornerstone in the study of geometric combinatorics, and it has found numerous applications in various areas of mathematics, including computational geometry, discrete geometry, and combinatorics. It has been instrumental in the development of efficient algorithms for solving problems involving incidences between points and curves.

In this chapter, we will first introduce the basic concepts and definitions related to the Pach-Sharir Theorem. We will then state the theorem in its full generality and discuss its implications. We will also explore some of the key techniques used in the proof of the theorem, including the use of combinatorial curves and the concept of incidence.

We will also discuss some of the key applications of the Pach-Sharir Theorem, including its role in the study of geometric configurations and its applications in computational geometry. We will also touch upon some of the open problems and future directions in the study of the Pach-Sharir Theorem.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid understanding of the Pach-Sharir Theorem and its role in geometric combinatorics. They will also be equipped with the necessary tools to explore further the fascinating world of geometric combinatorics.




### Subsection: 7.1a Definition and Statement

The Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves is a fundamental result in the field of geometric combinatorics. It provides a powerful tool for understanding the incidences between points and combinatorial curves in geometric configurations. The theorem is named after the Hungarian mathematicians Miklos Pach and Shimon Sharir, who first proved it in 1987.

#### 7.1a.1 Definition of Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem can be stated as follows:

Given a set of $n$ points in the plane and a set of $m$ combinatorial curves, the number of incidences between the points and the curves is at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3} + n + m)$.

Here, an incidence is defined as a point lying on a curve. The theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences, which is crucial for many applications in computational geometry.

#### 7.1a.2 Statement of Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has many important implications and applications. One of the key implications is that it provides a way to efficiently solve problems involving incidences between points and curves. For example, it can be used to find the shortest line segment connecting two points, or to find the shortest curve connecting two points.

The theorem also has applications in the study of geometric configurations. For instance, it can be used to prove theorems about the number of lines or curves in a configuration. It can also be used to study the complexity of geometric problems, such as the complexity of finding the intersection of two curves.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem and explore some of its key applications. We will also discuss some of the key techniques used in the proof, including the use of combinatorial curves and the concept of incidence.




#### 7.1b Proof and Derivations

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem is a complex and intricate process that involves a series of steps and derivations. In this section, we will outline the key steps of the proof and provide a detailed explanation of the derivations involved.

#### 7.1b.1 Key Steps of the Proof

The proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem can be broadly divided into three key steps:

1. **Reduction to a simpler problem:** The first step of the proof involves reducing the problem to a simpler form. This is achieved by considering only a subset of the points and curves that satisfy certain properties. The reduction allows us to focus on a smaller set of points and curves, making the problem more manageable.

2. **Application of the Erdős-Szekeres Theorem:** The second step of the proof involves applying the Erdős-Szekeres Theorem, a fundamental result in combinatorics that provides an upper bound on the number of incidences between points and lines. This step is crucial for establishing the upper bound on the number of incidences between points and curves.

3. **Analysis of the upper bound:** The final step of the proof involves analyzing the upper bound on the number of incidences established in the previous step. This involves using techniques from combinatorics and geometry to show that the upper bound is indeed the best possible.

#### 7.1b.2 Derivations

The derivations involved in the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem are based on a series of key identities and relations. These include the list of logarithmic identities, set identities and relations, and exterior calculus identities.

##### Logarithmic Identities

The logarithmic identities play a crucial role in the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem. For instance, the identity $\frac{1}{\frac{1}{\log_x(a)} + \frac{1}{\log_y(a)}} = \log_{xy}(a)$ is used to simplify certain expressions involving logarithms.

##### Set Identities and Relations

The set identities and relations are used to manipulate the sets of points and curves in the problem. For example, the identity $L \setminus (M \setminus R) 
&= (L \setminus M) \cup (L \cap R)$ is used to simplify certain set expressions.

##### Exterior Calculus Identities

The exterior calculus identities are used to manipulate the exterior products of one-forms. For instance, the identity $\alpha \wedge \beta = (-1)^{kl}\beta \wedge \alpha$ is used to simplify certain exterior products.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the details of these derivations and provide a step-by-step explanation of the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem.

#### 7.1c Applications and Examples

The Pach-Sharir Theorem on Incidences of Points and Combinatorial Curves has a wide range of applications in various fields, including computational geometry, combinatorics, and theoretical computer science. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the theorem in action.

##### Computational Geometry

In computational geometry, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is used to solve problems involving the incidence of points and curves. For instance, it can be used to find the shortest line segment connecting two points, or to determine the number of points lying on a given curve. The theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences, which can be used to design efficient algorithms for these problems.

##### Combinatorics

In combinatorics, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is used to study the structure of point sets and curve sets. For example, it can be used to prove theorems about the number of points lying on a given curve, or about the number of curves passing through a given point. The theorem also provides insights into the complexity of these structures, which can be useful in various applications.

##### Theoretical Computer Science

In theoretical computer science, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is used to study the complexity of geometric problems. For instance, it can be used to prove lower bounds on the complexity of problems involving the incidence of points and curves. The theorem also provides a framework for understanding the trade-offs between the number of points and curves and the complexity of the problem.

##### Example: Incidence of Points and Lines

Consider a set of $n$ points in the plane and a set of $m$ lines. The Pach-Sharir Theorem provides an upper bound on the number of incidences between these points and lines. This bound can be used to design an algorithm that, given a set of points and lines, finds the shortest line segment connecting two points. The algorithm can work by testing each line in turn and keeping track of the shortest line segment found so far. The Pach-Sharir Theorem ensures that this algorithm will terminate after at most $O(n^{2/3}m^{2/3} + n + m)$ steps.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the proof of the Pach-Sharir Theorem and explore some of the key techniques used in its derivation.




#### 7.1c Applications and Examples

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has a wide range of applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the theorem in action.

##### Applications in Computer Science

In computer science, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is used in the design and analysis of algorithms for various problems, such as range searching and nearest neighbor search. These problems involve finding the nearest point or the points within a given range to a query point. The theorem provides a theoretical upper bound on the number of incidences between points and curves, which can be used to design efficient algorithms for these problems.

For example, consider the problem of range searching in a two-dimensional plane. Given a set of points $P$ and a query point $q$, the goal is to find all the points in $P$ that lie within a given range $R$ of $q$. The Pach-Sharir Theorem can be used to provide an upper bound on the number of incidences between the query point $q$ and the points in $P$, which can then be used to design an efficient algorithm for this problem.

##### Examples in Mathematics

This can be useful in studying the complexity of the curve $C$ and its relationship with the set $P$.

##### Examples in Engineering

In engineering, the Pach-Sharir Theorem is used in the design and analysis of structures. For example, in civil engineering, the theorem can be used to analyze the stability of structures under load. For example, consider a beam under load. The Pach-Sharir Theorem can be used to provide an upper bound on the number of incidences between the points on the beam and the points on the load. This can then be used to design a beam that can withstand the load without failing.

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of combinatorial curves and their applications. They are a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics, a field that studies the interplay between geometry and combinatorics.

The study of combinatorial curves is not just an abstract mathematical exercise. It has a wide range of applications in various fields, including computer graphics, computational geometry, and algorithm design. #### 7.2a Definitions and Concepts

In this section, we will introduce some key concepts and definitions that are essential for understanding the various crossing numbers and their applications.

##### Crossing Numbers

A crossing number is a measure of the complexity of a geometric object, such as a curve or a surface. It is defined as the maximum number of incidences between the points and the segments of the object. In other words, it is the maximum number of times a point can intersect a segment of the object.

##### Incidences

An incidence is a point-segment intersection. In other words, it is a point that lies on a segment. The number of incidences between a set of points and a set of segments is a measure of the complexity of the intersection between these two sets.

##### Combinatorial Curves

A combinatorial curve is a geometric object that can be represented as a sequence of points and segments. It is a fundamental concept in geometric combinatorics and is used to study the complexity of geometric objects.

##### Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem is a fundamental result in geometric combinatorics that provides a theoretical upper bound on the number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves. It is named after the mathematicians Miklos Pach and Shimon Sharir, who first proved it.

##### Applications of Crossing Numbers

Crossing numbers have a wide range of applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering. In computer science, they are used in the design and analysis of algorithms for problems such as range searching and nearest neighbor search. In mathematics, they are used in the study of combinatorial curves and surfaces. In engineering, they are used in the design and analysis of structures and systems.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the various crossing numbers and their applications, starting with the Pach-Sharir Theorem.

#### 7.2b Properties and Theorems

In this section, we will explore some key properties and theorems related to crossing numbers and their applications.

##### Properties of Crossing Numbers

1. Crossing numbers are always non-negative integers. This is because the maximum number of incidences between a set of points and a set of segments is always finite.

2. The crossing number of a combinatorial curve is a measure of its complexity. A curve with a higher crossing number is generally more complex than a curve with a lower crossing number.

3. The crossing number of a combinatorial curve is invariant under translation and rotation. This means that the complexity of a curve does not change if we move or rotate it.

##### The Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem is a fundamental result in geometric combinatorics that provides a theoretical upper bound on the number of incidences between points and combinatorial curves. It is named after the mathematicians Miklos Pach and Shimon Sharir, who first proved it.

The theorem states that for any set of $n$ points and $m$ segments in the plane, the number of incidences between these points and segments is at most $O(n^{1/2}m^{1/2})$. This result is significant because it provides a theoretical upper bound on the complexity of the intersection between points and segments.

##### Applications of Crossing Numbers and the Pach-Sharir Theorem

The Pach-Sharir Theorem has a wide range of applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering. In computer science, it is used in the design and analysis of algorithms for problems such as range searching and nearest neighbor search. In mathematics, it is used in the study of combinatorial curves and surfaces. In engineering, it is used in the design and analysis of structures and systems.

For example, in computer science, the Pach-Sharir Theorem can be used to design efficient algorithms for range searching and nearest neighbor search problems. In mathematics, it can be used to study the complexity of combinatorial curves and surfaces. In engineering, it can be used to analyze the stability of structures and systems.
