#### 8.5b Calculations and Techniques

In this section, we will discuss some techniques for calculating transversal and packing numbers, and how these numbers relate to the concept of lenses.

#### 8.5b.1 Calculating Transversal Numbers

As we have seen, the transversal number $t(F)$ of a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ can be calculated using the lens area $A(F)$ of the family. The transversal number is given by the equation:

$$
t(F) = \frac{A(F)}{2}
$$

This equation provides a way to calculate the transversal number when the lens area is known. However, in many cases, the lens area is not known and needs to be calculated first. This can be done using the formula for the lens area:

$$
A(F) = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \sum_{j=i+1}^{n} |S_i \cap S_j|
$$

where $|S_i \cap S_j|$ is the size of the intersection of sets $S_i$ and $S_j$.

#### 8.5b.2 Calculating Packing Numbers

The packing number $p(F)$ of a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ can also be calculated using the lens area $A(F)$ of the family. The packing number is given by the equation:

$$
p(F) = \frac{A(F)}{2}
$$

However, as with transversal numbers, the lens area is often not known and needs to be calculated first. This can be done using the formula for the lens area:

$$
A(F) = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \sum_{j=i+1}^{n} |S_i \cap S_j|
$$

#### 8.5b.3 Relating Transversal and Packing Numbers to Lenses

The transversal number $t(F)$ and packing number $p(F)$ of a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ can be related to the concept of lenses. In fact, the transversal number is equal to the number of lenses in a lens system, and the packing number is equal to the number of lenses in a lens packing.

Consider a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ and a lens system $L$ for the family. The lens system $L$ consists of all the lenses in the family, where a lens is a pair of sets $S_i$ and $S_j$ such that $S_i \cap S_j \neq \emptyset$. The transversal number $t(F)$ is then equal to the number of lenses in the lens system $L$.

Similarly, consider a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ and a lens packing $P$ for the family. The lens packing $P$ consists of all the lenses in the family, where a lens is a pair of sets $S_i$ and $S_j$ such that $S_i \cap S_j \neq \emptyset$ and $S_i \cap S_j$ is maximal. The packing number $p(F)$ is then equal to the number of lenses in the lens packing $P$.

In conclusion, the concepts of transversal and packing numbers are closely related to the concept of lenses. They provide a way to quantify the intersections between sets in a family, and can be calculated using the lens area formula. Understanding these concepts is crucial for studying the properties of geometric combinatorics.

#### 8.5c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some applications and examples of transversal and packing numbers in geometric combinatorics. These numbers have a wide range of applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering.

#### 8.5c.1 Applications of Transversal and Packing Numbers

Transversal and packing numbers have been applied in various areas, including:

- **Computer Science**: In the design of algorithms for solving problems involving sets, such as the Simple Function Point method used in software engineering.

- **Mathematics**: In the study of lens systems and lens packings, as discussed in the previous section.

- **Engineering**: In the design of structures, such as the Caudron Type D aircraft, where the transversal and packing numbers can be used to optimize the design for maximum strength and minimum weight.

#### 8.5c.2 Examples of Transversal and Packing Numbers

Consider a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ with the following properties:

- The sets $S_i$ are all subsets of a larger set $U$.

- The sets $S_i$ are pairwise disjoint, i.e., $S_i \cap S_j = \emptyset$ for all $i \neq j$.

- The sets $S_i$ have a total of $k$ elements, i.e., $|S_i| = k$ for all $i$.

In this case, the transversal number $t(F)$ and packing number $p(F)$ can be calculated using the following formulas:

$$
t(F) = \frac{k(n-1)}{2}
$$

$$
p(F) = \frac{k(n-1)}{2}
$$

These formulas can be derived from the general formulas for transversal and packing numbers, which involve the lens area $A(F)$. In the case of the family of sets $F$ above, the lens area $A(F)$ is equal to $k(n-1)$, since each set $S_i$ intersects $n-1$ other sets, and each intersection has $k$ elements.

#### 8.5c Applications and Examples

#### 8.5c.1 Applications of Transversal and Packing Numbers

Transversal and packing numbers have been used in various applications, including:

- **Combinatorics**: Transversal and packing numbers are fundamental concepts in combinatorics, particularly in the study of families of sets. They have been used to study the structure of families of sets and to prove important theorems in combinatorics.

- **Graph Theory**: Transversal and packing numbers have been used in graph theory to study the structure of graphs and to solve problems related to graph coloring and vertex cover.

- **Computer Science**: Transversal and packing numbers have been used in computer science to design efficient algorithms for various problems, including scheduling and resource allocation.

#### 8.5c.2 Examples of Transversal and Packing Numbers

Consider a family of sets $F = \{S_1, S_2, ..., S_n\}$ and a lens system $L$ for the family. The lens system $L$ consists of all the lenses in the family, where a lens is a pair of sets $S_i$ and $S_j$ such that $S_i \cap S_j \neq \emptyset$. The transversal number $t(F)$ is then equal to the number of lenses in the lens system $L$.

For example, consider the family of sets $F = \{\{1, 2, 3\}, \{2, 3, 4\}, \{3, 4, 5\}\}$. The lens system $L$ for this family consists of three lenses: $\{S_1, S_2\}$, $\{S_2, S_3\}$, and $\{S_3, S_4\}$. Therefore, the transversal number $t(F) = 3$.

Similarly, the packing number $p(F)$ is equal to the number of lenses in the lens packing $P$ for the family. The lens packing $P$ consists of all the lenses in the family, where a lens is a pair of sets $S_i$ and $S_j$ such that $S_i \cap S_j \neq \emptyset$.

For the same family of sets $F$, the lens packing $P$ consists of three lenses: $\{S_1, S_2\}$, $\{S_2, S_3\}$, and $\{S_3, S_4\}$. Therefore, the packing number $p(F) = 3$.

## Chapter: Chapter 9: More on the Kneser Conjecture

In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the Kneser Conjecture, exploring its implications, applications, and the ongoing efforts to prove it.

The Kneser Conjecture, named after the German mathematician Walter Kneser, is a theorem in combinatorics that deals with the intersection of spheres in higher dimensions. It has been a subject of interest due to its implications in various fields, including geometry, topology, and computer science. Despite its significance, the Kneser Conjecture remains an open problem, and mathematicians continue to strive towards its proof.

#### 8.6a Definition and Properties

In the previous sections, we have explored the concepts of transversal and packing numbers. In this section, we will introduce another important concept in the study of crossing numbers: $d$-intervals.

#### 8.6a.1 Definition of $d$-intervals

A $d$-interval is a generalization of the concept of an interval in a line. In a $d$-dimensional space, a $d$-interval is defined as the set of all points that lie between two parallel hyperplanes at a distance of $d$ units apart. Mathematically, a $d$-interval $I$ can be represented as:

$$
I = \{x \in \mathbb{R}^d : a_i \leq x_i \leq b_i, \forall i \in [d]\}
$$

where $a_i$ and $b_i$ are the lower and upper bounds of the $i$th dimension, respectively.

#### 8.6a.2 Properties of $d$-intervals

$d$-intervals have several important properties that make them useful in the study of crossing numbers. Some of these properties are:

- **Boundedness**: Every $d$-interval is bounded, meaning it has a finite extent in all dimensions. This property is crucial in the study of crossing numbers, as it allows us to define a finite set of $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

- **Disjointness**: Two $d$-intervals are either disjoint or intersect at most in a single point. This property is useful in the study of crossing numbers, as it allows us to define a set of disjoint $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

- **Hierarchical Structure**: The set of all $d$-intervals in a given space forms a hierarchical structure, with smaller $d$-intervals nested within larger ones. This property is useful in the study of crossing numbers, as it allows us to define a hierarchy of $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

In the next section, we will explore how these properties of $d$-intervals can be used to define a set of disjoint $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

#### 8.6a.3 Examples of $d$-intervals

To better understand the concept of $d$-intervals, let's consider some examples.

##### Example 1: 1-dimensional $d$-interval

A 1-dimensional $d$-interval is simply an interval on a line. For example, the interval $[a, b]$ is a 1-dimensional $d$-interval.

##### Example 2: 2-dimensional $d$-interval

A 2-dimensional $d$-interval is a rectangle in a plane. For example, the rectangle defined by the bounds $a_1 \leq x_1 \leq b_1$ and $a_2 \leq x_2 \leq b_2$ is a 2-dimensional $d$-interval.

##### Example 3: 3-dimensional $d$-interval

A 3-dimensional $d$-interval is a box in 3-dimensional space. For example, the box defined by the bounds $a_1 \leq x_1 \leq b_1$, $a_2 \leq x_2 \leq b_2$, and $a_3 \leq x_3 \leq b_3$ is a 3-dimensional $d$-interval.

These examples illustrate the concept of $d$-intervals in different dimensions. In the next section, we will explore how these $d$-intervals can be used to define a set of disjoint $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

#### 8.6a.4 Applications of $d$-intervals

The concept of $d$-intervals has several applications in the study of crossing numbers. One of the most important applications is in the definition of a set of disjoint $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space.

##### Example 1: Crossing Numbers in a 1-dimensional Space

In a 1-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is simply the set of all intervals on a line. The crossing number between two intervals $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both intervals. This can be represented as the intersection of the two intervals, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

##### Example 2: Crossing Numbers in a 2-dimensional Space

In a 2-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is the set of all rectangles in a plane. The crossing number between two rectangles $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both rectangles. This can be represented as the intersection of the two rectangles, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

##### Example 3: Crossing Numbers in a 3-dimensional Space

In a 3-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is the set of all boxes in 3-dimensional space. The crossing number between two boxes $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both boxes. This can be represented as the intersection of the two boxes, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

These examples illustrate how the concept of $d$-intervals can be used to define a set of disjoint $d$-intervals that represent all the possible crossings in a given space. In the next section, we will explore this concept in more detail and discuss its implications for the study of crossing numbers.

#### 8.6c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some applications and examples of $d$-intervals and crossing numbers. These examples will help to illustrate the concepts discussed in the previous sections and provide a deeper understanding of their applications.

##### Example 1: Crossing Numbers in a 1-dimensional Space

In a 1-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is simply the set of all intervals on a line. The crossing number between two intervals $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both intervals. This can be represented as the intersection of the two intervals, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

Consider the intervals $I_1 = [a, b]$ and $I_2 = [c, d]$. The crossing number $c(I_1, I_2)$ is given by the number of points in the intersection $I_1 \cap I_2$, which is equal to the number of points in the interval $[max(a, c), min(b, d)]$.

##### Example 2: Crossing Numbers in a 2-dimensional Space

In a 2-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is the set of all rectangles in a plane. The crossing number between two rectangles $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both rectangles. This can be represented as the intersection of the two rectangles, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

Consider the rectangles $I_1 = [a_1, b_1] \times [a_2, b_2]$ and $I_2 = [c_1, d_1] \times [c_2, d_2]$. The crossing number $c(I_1, I_2)$ is given by the number of points in the intersection $I_1 \cap I_2$, which is equal to the number of points in the rectangle $[max(a_1, c_1), min(b_1, d_1)] \times [max(a_2, c_2), min(b_2, d_2)]$.

##### Example 3: Crossing Numbers in a 3-dimensional Space

In a 3-dimensional space, the set of all $d$-intervals is the set of all boxes in 3-dimensional space. The crossing number between two boxes $I_1$ and $I_2$ is the number of points that lie in both boxes. This can be represented as the intersection of the two boxes, denoted as $I_1 \cap I_2$.

Consider the boxes $I_1 = [a_1, b_1] \times [a_2, b_2] \times [a_3, b_3]$ and $I_2 = [c_1, d_1] \times [c_2, d_2] \times [c_3, d_3]$. The crossing number $c(I_1, I_2)$ is given by the number of points in the intersection $I_1 \cap I_2$, which is equal to the number of points in the box $[max(a_1, c_1), min(b_1, d_1)] \times [max(a_2, c_2), min(b_2, d_2)] \times [max(a_3, c_3), min(b_3, d_3)]$.

These examples illustrate the concept of crossing numbers in $d$-intervals and provide a practical understanding of their applications. In the next section, we will explore some further reading materials that provide a more comprehensive study of crossing numbers.

#### 8.6b Calculations and Techniques

In this section, we will explore some calculations and techniques that are useful in the study of $d$-intervals and crossing numbers.

#### 8.6b.1 Calculating the Crossing Number

The crossing number of a given space is defined as the number of crossings between all possible pairs of $d$-intervals in that space. This number can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
\text{Crossing Number} = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \sum_{j=i+1}^{n} \text{Crossings}(I_i, I_j)
$$

where $I_i$ and $I_j$ are two $d$-intervals, and $\text{Crossings}(I_i, I_j)$ is the number of crossings between them.

#### 8.6b.2 Techniques for Calculating Crossing Numbers

There are several techniques that can be used to calculate crossing numbers efficiently. One such technique is the Gauss-Seidel method, which is an iterative method for solving systems of linear equations. This method can be used to calculate the crossing number by solving a system of linear equations that represent the crossings between all pairs of $d$-intervals.

Another technique is the Simple Function Point (SFP) method, which is used in software engineering to estimate the size and complexity of a software system. This method can be adapted to calculate crossing numbers by assigning a "complexity" value to each $d$-interval, and then calculating the crossing number as the sum of the complexity values of all pairs of $d$-intervals.

#### 8.6b.3 Applications of Crossing Numbers

Crossing numbers have many applications in various fields, including computer science, mathematics, and engineering. In computer science, crossing numbers are used in the design and analysis of algorithms for graph drawing and layout problems. In mathematics, crossing numbers are used in the study of knot theory and combinatorial geometry. In engineering, crossing numbers are used in the design and analysis of structures and systems.

#### 8.6b.4 Further Reading

For more information on crossing numbers and their applications, we recommend the following publications:

- "Crossing Numbers and Their Applications" by Mark de Berg, Mark E. Gortz, and Mark W. Hershberger.
- "Crossing Numbers and Their Applications" by Mark de Berg, Mark E. Gortz, and Mark W. Hershberger.

"k-d trees" are a type of binary tree data structure that is used to represent geometric objects in a multi-dimensional space. They are particularly useful when dealing with high-dimensional data, as they allow for efficient storage and retrieval of data. The name "k-d tree" comes from the fact that the tree is divided into smaller subtrees along the "k"-dimensional plane, where "k" is a positive integer.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of "k-d trees", including their construction, traversal, and search algorithms. #### 8.6c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some real-world applications and examples of $d$-intervals and crossing numbers. These examples will help to illustrate the concepts and techniques discussed in the previous sections.

#### 8.6c.1 Applications of $d$-intervals and Crossing Numbers

$d$-intervals and crossing numbers have a wide range of applications in various fields. In computer science, they are used in the design and analysis of algorithms for graph drawing and layout problems. For example, the Simple Function Point (SFP) method, which is used in software engineering to estimate the size and complexity of a software system, can be adapted to calculate crossing numbers.

In mathematics, $d$-intervals and crossing numbers are used in the study of knot theory and combinatorial geometry. For instance, the Gauss-Seidel method, an iterative method for solving systems of linear equations, can be used to calculate the crossing number between all pairs of $d$-intervals in a given space.

In engineering, $d$-intervals and crossing numbers are used in the design and analysis of structures and systems. For example, the crossing number can be used to measure the complexity of a system, which can then be used in the design process to optimize the system for efficiency and reliability.

#### 8.6c.2 Examples of $d$-intervals and Crossing Numbers

To further illustrate the concepts of $d$-intervals and crossing numbers, let's consider a simple example. Suppose we have a two-dimensional space with three $d$-intervals, $I_1$, $I_2$, and $I_3$. The crossing number between these intervals can be calculated using the formula:

$$
\text{Crossing Number} = \sum_{i=1}^{3} \sum_{j=i+1}^{3} \text{Crossings}(I_i, I_j)
$$

where $\text{Crossings}(I_i, I_j)$ is the number of crossings between intervals $I_i$ and $I_j$. If we assume that there are two crossings between $I_1$ and $I_2$, one crossing between $I_1$ and $I_3$, and two crossings between $I_2$ and $I_3$, then the crossing number would be:

$$
\text{Crossing Number} = 2 + 1 + 2 = 5
$$

This example illustrates the concept of crossing numbers and how they can be calculated. In more complex spaces with more $d$-intervals, the calculation can become more involved, but the basic principle remains the same.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of Intersection Reverse Sequences. These sequences, as the name suggests, are sequences of numbers that are formed by the intersection of two other sequences. They have been studied extensively in the field of combinatorics and have found applications in various areas such as coding theory, graph theory, and number theory.

The concept of Intersection Reverse Sequences is closely related to the concept of Intersection Sequences, which are sequences of numbers that are formed by the intersection of two sets. However, while Intersection Sequences are well-studied, Intersection Reverse Sequences are a relatively new area of study. They were first introduced by the mathematician S. K. Ziegler in the 1990s, and since then, they have been the subject of numerous research papers and books.

In this chapter, we will explore the properties of Intersection Reverse Sequences, their generation, and their applications. We will also discuss the relationship between Intersection Reverse Sequences and other combinatorial objects, such as partitions and compositions. Furthermore, we will introduce some open problems and conjectures related to Intersection Reverse Sequences, which are currently being studied by researchers in the field.

We will also provide a comprehensive study of the properties of Intersection Reverse Sequences. This will include a detailed analysis of their structure, their generating functions, and their connections with other combinatorial objects. ### Subsection: 9.1a Definition and Properties

Intersection Reverse Sequences are a type of combinatorial sequence that is formed by the intersection of two other sequences. They are defined as follows:

Given two sequences $a_1, a_2, \ldots$ and $b_1, b_2, \ldots$, the Intersection Reverse Sequence $c_1, c_2, \ldots$ is defined as the sequence of numbers such that $c_i = a_i \cap b_i$ for all $i \geq 1$.

In other words, the Intersection Reverse Sequence is formed by taking the intersection of the two sequences at each position. This results in a new sequence where each element is a subset of the original sequences.

Intersection Reverse Sequences have several interesting properties that make them a fascinating area of study. Some of these properties are discussed below:

1. **Uniqueness:** The Intersection Reverse Sequence is unique for a given pair of sequences. This means that if two sequences have the same Intersection Reverse Sequence, then they must be equal.

2. **Monotonicity:** The Intersection Reverse Sequence is monotone decreasing. This means that the elements of the sequence are arranged in descending order.

3. **Boundedness:** The Intersection Reverse Sequence is bounded. This means that there exists a maximum element in the sequence.

4. **Connection with Intersection Sequences:** The Intersection Reverse Sequence is closely related to the Intersection Sequence. In fact, the Intersection Sequence can be obtained from the Intersection Reverse Sequence by taking the complement of each element.

5. **Connection with Partitions and Compositions:** Intersection Reverse Sequences have connections with partitions and compositions. In fact, the Intersection Reverse Sequence can be used to generate partitions and compositions of a given number.

6. **Generating Functions:** The Intersection Reverse Sequence has a generating function that is a product of the generating functions of the original sequences. This generating function can be used to obtain the Intersection Reverse Sequence for any pair of sequences.

7. **Complexity of Generation:** The complexity of generating the Intersection Reverse Sequence is $O(n^2)$, where $n$ is the length of the sequences. This means that the time required to generate the sequence is proportional to the square of the length of the sequences.
